<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2a32ea67-9224-44f5-b737-332dd26f2fac'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer16918344690371915699">With ten stories that move from the barrios of the Dominican Republic to the struggling urban communities of New Jersey, Junot Diaz makes his remarkable debut. Diaz's work is unflinching and strong, and these stories crackle with an electric sense of discovery. Diaz evokes a world in which fathers are gone, mothers fight with grim determination for their families and thems</span>
  <span id="freeText16918344690371915699" style="display:none">With ten stories that move from the barrios of the Dominican Republic to the struggling urban communities of New Jersey, Junot Diaz makes his remarkable debut. Diaz's work is unflinching and strong, and these stories crackle with an electric sense of discovery. Diaz evokes a world in which fathers are gone, mothers fight with grim determination for their families and themselves, and the next generation inherits the casual cruelty, devastating ambivalence, and knowing humor of lives circumscribed by poverty and uncertainty. In <b>Drown</b>, Diaz has harnessed the rhythms of anger and release, frustration and joy, to indelible effect.</span>

<div id="review_14381046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14381046" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14381046?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14381046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4257005588500551459">As when you're listening to some old piece of music you never thought much of, it could be a long ago seemingly throwaway pop dance number like This Old Heart of Mine by the Isley Brothers, or some slyer more college-degreed album track like (let's say) Life During Wartime by Talking Heads, and you suddenly jump up and think but - but really, this is a masterpiece! - it's not just another painting-by-numbers from Motown, it's not just another sneery too-clever construction you skip while you're </span>
  <span id="freeText4257005588500551459" style="display:none">As when you're listening to some old piece of music you never thought much of, it could be a long ago seemingly throwaway pop dance number like This Old Heart of Mine by the Isley Brothers, or some slyer more college-degreed album track like (let's say) Life During Wartime by Talking Heads, and you suddenly jump up and think but - but really, this is a masterpiece! - it's not just another painting-by-numbers from Motown, it's not just another sneery too-clever construction you skip while you're trying to find Once in a Lifetime, Juno Diaz' tales from the front line of squalour and immigration aren't just another vicarious thrillseeking tour of Poverty-and-Ignorance Hell, not just another wound-baring stigmata-showing howl from yet more people from yet another abyss you wish you didn't know about. They're that too, just like This Old Heart of Mine is a great dance number, but there's this thing called an authentic voice, or whatever the term is. Now this is a thing you can't buy with money. It's where no one but this particular author would know how to describe this person or that circumstance, this pain or that crime, the unique inside the generic. A twist of brain and language, and eyes and heart and blood wrapped round it too. Junot Diaz is the author, Drown is the book.<br /><br /> The best story is the last and longest, but only because of what went before. Gloomily overhanging the earlier tales of kids trying to grow up in the Dominican Republic like a cloud full of bad rain is the father who left for America, who never came back, and who stopped sending money, and who never sent for his family to join him. What a shit! We get used to the banal outrage of not knowing what this useless fool did when he got there, what was his big fat excuse, how could he abandon his wife and kids, etc etc so that there's a real frisson when we discover that the last 47 pages will answer all these questions, and in a great reversal of perspective, you find yourself looking out of this bad man's face, sleeping in his cockroach rooms and working his 16 hour shifts and against your whole will, you understand.<br /> Great stuff, and not one wasted word. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4257005588500551459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2132841" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2132841" />
    <a title="Myfanwy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/140287-myfanwy"><img alt="Myfanwy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402004777p2/140287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2132841?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Myfanwy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Myfanwy" href="/user/show/140287-myfanwy">Myfanwy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2132841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12016742614928719156">There are several recurrent themes running through this collection (the lost father, the regained father, the lost love, brotherhood, betrayal--often sexual) but the one I found most striking was that of facelessness. <br/><br/>You would think that facelessness is synonymous with invisibility, but here it is not. There is something within that facelessness, which makes the person all the more visible--scorned, pitied, hated, feared, and by some, treated with great kindness. The faced want the faceless to </span>
  <span id="freeText12016742614928719156" style="display:none">There are several recurrent themes running through this collection (the lost father, the regained father, the lost love, brotherhood, betrayal--often sexual) but the one I found most striking was that of facelessness. <br /><br />You would think that facelessness is synonymous with invisibility, but here it is not. There is something within that facelessness, which makes the person all the more visible--scorned, pitied, hated, feared, and by some, treated with great kindness. The faced want the faceless to be gone for good because they represent the worst fear: That you, too, might one day suffer this fate where all that defines you to the outside world is stripped away, where you are a stranger in a strange land--where you are unloved and unlovable. <br /><br />"Ysrael" is the boy with no face, his face having been mostly chewed off by a pig when he was an infant. Because of this he wears a mask and awaits a humanitarian intervention in which doctors in Canada are meant to restore his face. But this day never seems to come and he is scorned and beaten, but he is also an object of intense interest. There is something about him that fascinates the other boys; if only they could just see behind his mask. But even when they do, it infuriates them, repulses them. There is nothing in seeing his face that makes them feel better about themselves. It only makes them feel worse, more powerless. <br /><br />Then when the reader sees the world from his point of view in "No Face," we understand that though he is deformed and maligned there is still great hope and beauty in his world, though he might not realize it. There is something strong deep within that will keep him alive despite the obstacles. He is a survivor. He will run. <br /><br />So Ysrael stands for the best hope of all of the faceless within these stories--and the message is to keep going, keep running, keep moving forward no matter how people will push you down and try to keep you from being seen. <br /><br />In that, a book, which might otherwise be bleak, I found quite hopeful. And so, in the end, what you have is a collection of stories that are beautiful, necessary, and heartbreaking. Read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12016742614928719156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103319396?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103319396" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15250273475941610576">Yeah, yeah, once you get the Pulitzer your earlier works may be scavenged & retold and republished and possibly (if you ARE the literatti:) re-read.<br/><br/>This is my first foray into the infamous short story terrain animated by Junot Diaz. Yes- his first novel was outstanding, & just its level of genius is constantly debated: everyone is aware it's really f***in' good.<br/><br/>"Drown" is endearing. An autobiography of ten short stories that are exquisite maps into the writer's early life in the D.R. and in Amer</span>
  <span id="freeText15250273475941610576" style="display:none">Yeah, yeah, once you get the Pulitzer your earlier works may be scavenged &amp; retold and republished and possibly (if you ARE the literatti:) re-read.<br /><br />This is my first foray into the infamous short story terrain animated by Junot Diaz. Yes- his first novel was outstanding, &amp; just its level of genius is constantly debated: everyone is aware it's really f***in' good.<br /><br />"Drown" is endearing. An autobiography of ten short stories that are exquisite maps into the writer's early life in the D.R. and in America. Junot Diaz is the latino equivalent of David Sedaris. Each one of his vignettes is a colorful &amp; juicy morsel: word association: Starburst candy. While his story is his own, Diaz asks you to empathize with him and relate. This is extremely doable.<br /><br />I am aware that Junot Diaz is a super competent storyteller, but he does subscribe to this Dominican Man attitudes toward women and about how owning things (including, well, women) makes you a man. He endorses this mysogynistic attitude but he is more like a mirror in this way--extremely unapologetic and still personal.<br /><br />Highlight: the first tale is harsh and sad. The protagonist is evil and behaves contrary to how a hero usually performs. But the penultimate story revisits the little boy from the first, "No Face", (1/5th of the book is about the little disfigured boy, and you know the weight of this in the author's psyche) and at this moment you see magic materialize: a writer's guilt leads to some true immortal form (the best type, in my opinion,) of atonement.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15250273475941610576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1391018825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10511269702121429233">There's this white boy in the class that assigned this collection taking pot shots at it for misogyny, which is real easy when you're white and male and your eyes glaze over how deeply white girls and their white skin and their white features inspire both veneration and self-hatred. Objectification, to an extent, but when white's the standard of beauty and safety and the Dominican Republic's the name of the game, either you talk imperialism and intercommunity issues or you're just another coloni</span>
  <span id="freeText10511269702121429233" style="display:none">There's this white boy in the class that assigned this collection taking pot shots at it for misogyny, which is real easy when you're white and male and your eyes glaze over how deeply white girls and their white skin and their white features inspire both veneration and self-hatred. Objectification, to an extent, but when white's the standard of beauty and safety and the Dominican Republic's the name of the game, either you talk imperialism and intercommunity issues or you're just another colonial savior brat looking to save the brown women from the brown men in the name of divide and conquer. Besides, the gynephobic violence might be there, but I've read enough works that glorify that sort of shit to know DÃ­az isn't doing the same. The main short story cycle character Yunior doesn't go around blubbering at all the bad things he sees, but he does take it in without self-reflexive excuses or the defensive bravado so commonly known as masculinity. It's harder for me to poke at, as neither gender nor race are a common factor between me and him, but the normalization of hatred of women in literature leaves a bad taste that this particular collection did not spawn, so of course I have to peer at this result and wonder why.<br /><br />I'm glad I didn't follow the class itinerary of reading only six or so of the ten, cause the nice and neat line of the beginning story and the titular story and the ending "this is what it's sorta all about" story is perfect for an MFA program and horrible for actually getting a sense of what is going on. Proust in the time of race and heroin. Sentiment in the time of the death of English dominance and the rise of destabilization of the canon. The homeland's death, the way out's denigration, the destination might be life but there's way too many white supremacist slags running things for it to be anything other than a capitalistic jumpstart and/or deathtrap. Emotional bonds are a pain. Sexuality's a pain. The acrid freedom of individuals in the US is on one hand the collective familial unit of man of the household Dominican Republic is on the other, and both will never be an option so long as gringa serves a conceptual purpose of sociopolitical self-defense. <br /><br />If you're like me, a member of a white family stronghold living in the US, at one point in your life you <i>hated</i> these people. You <i>loathed</i> them. Never mind the billionaires buying up the legislation for fucking up those countries south of the border and the Gulf of Mexico even more, there's an immigrant who only speaks Spanish fifty feet away. Yeah it's a pain when people all over the world are headed towards your door, but that's what you get for always winning, always killing, always conquering, all over the world. All the time.<br /><br />It's a mess, and I'm not going to say I adored the abusive relationship of this collection's "Aurora", but you don't boil anything down to a single issue and flip it off accordingly. You can if that one thing inspires enough virulent disgust, but not before running through all the context first. In my case, there's a Dominican-American kid with a heart rocketing around these gritty and lovelocked pages, so this is not the kind of player on which I can lay the blame of the game.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13077040523476159966">It's easy to get so caught up in the misogyny of this story that you miss the point, but How to Date a Brown Girl is not just what it seems on the surface. It's not just a teenage playboy giving advice on how to get in the pants of a black girl, brown girl, white girl, or halfie. Junot Diaz always delves deeper than that. He shows us the protagonist's vulnerability through the boy's own attempts at seeming macho. <br/><br/>This story is mostly about a young man's personal self-hatred and the shame he has </span>
  <span id="freeText13077040523476159966" style="display:none">It's easy to get so caught up in the misogyny of this story that you miss the point, but How to Date a Brown Girl is not just what it seems on the surface. It's not just a teenage playboy giving advice on how to get in the pants of a black girl, brown girl, white girl, or halfie. Junot Diaz always delves deeper than that. He shows us the protagonist's vulnerability through the boy's own attempts at seeming macho. <br /><br />This story is mostly about a young man's personal self-hatred and the shame he has of his identity. People of color (often unknowingly) spend a lot of time "apologizing" for their culture, skin color, hair type, vernacular, socio-economic status; the way we walk, talk and exist in the world. That's what How to Date a Brown Girl is about. A teen who comes off as a self-absorbed, chauvinistic asshole when he's really just another damaged brown boy trying to wear a mask for the world. If the protagonist were self-aware, he might label his instructions, "How to Not be Yourself So That Others Deem You Worthy."</span>

<div id="review_106445298" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106445298" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2447803-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106445298?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/2447803-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio book " href="/book/show/4459294-drown">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106445298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4715406082901387219">I can't do it. I can't listen to books on tape. <br/><br/>Listening to tapes allows me one opportunity--one time only--to experience the writing. That's not my paradigm. It's not the way I've grown to experience books. I need to look at the physical words--they mean something. I need to reread sentences and paragraphs. I need to touch pages and manipulate the weight and rectilinear dimensions of the book. I need to interpret and define and orient and catalog the story into my own retrievable cranial netwo</span>
  <span id="freeText4715406082901387219" style="display:none">I can't do it. I can't listen to books on tape. <br /><br />Listening to tapes allows me one opportunity--one time only--to experience the writing. That's not my paradigm. It's not the way I've grown to experience books. I need to look at the physical words--they mean something. I need to reread sentences and paragraphs. I need to touch pages and manipulate the weight and rectilinear dimensions of the book. I need to interpret and define and orient and catalog the story into my own retrievable cranial network. I need to stop and think about what I just read. I need to see my progress through the book, the dogeared pages. I need to reference an earlier exchange of dialogue. I need to put the book down and figure out which of the characters in my life most closely represent the characters I'm reading in the book. Those are the kinds of connections, fiction to real life, in situ, that I've been doing since I began reading Hardy Boy mysteries at the age of 10. It's how my brain has worked since I was begat. And then, all those connections (both conscious and synaptic), and curious definitions, and mental images get all mixed together in the gray matter, and out through my mouth and typing fingers, comes a rather normal human perspective of a book that I can then talk about with others. Your reading experience, I suspect, occurs in a similar manner.<br /><br />But not with books on tape. And not for me. After a 14 hour driving trip listening to <i>Drown</i>, I'm not prepared to write a review. Somebody else read this book; I merely heard it. It was <i>their</i> voice and <i>their</i> intonation and <i>their</i> glottalizations and <i>their</i> cuss words. Not mine. It was not my experience. <br /><br />I can no more write reviews of movies for the same reason. I don't have a relationship to the physicality of a DVD. I don't understand why I can listen to a lecture (or a performance or music or see a painting) and retain the information I need, but I cannot have books read to me. Please explain. It's a phenomenon I can't figure out.<br /><br /><i>Drown</i> sounded like a decent book, but I can't be sure. In the same obsessive compulsive way that I have to check the door to ensure it's locked instead of trusting you to have locked it correctly, I need to read a book, not have you read it to me. Reading is MY time; it's not OUR time.<br /><br />Story, story, story,<br />Does that vanity plate really say 'DUMP'...?<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />Damn, I just dropped a handful of sunflower seeds, did they get into the...<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />Why don't you drive, and pass me, and stop staring so long...<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />Exit 264, was that the exit with the cheapest gas last time...?<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />So, 270 more miles at 70 mph will/should get me there by...<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />An RV towing a boat towing a jeep with 5 bicycles on a rear rack; man, if that thing rolled...<br />...oh, story, story, story,<br />I gotta pee...<br />...oh, story, story, story.<br /><br />I abstain from writing a review until I can read this book. So, in the meantime, I default to an irresolute 3 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4715406082901387219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2368246955563303570">I was lucky enough to have seen Junot Diaz read, and that cabrÃ²n was hilarious! His talk was fresh, lewd, direct, sly, sweet, and honest. Exactly like his writing. He spoke of how Hip Hop had informed his life and work, and how a writer must use experience to shape their art; auto-biography and fiction helix together.   <span id="freeText2368246955563303570" style="display:none">I was lucky enough to have seen Junot Diaz read, and that cabrÃ²n was hilarious! His talk was fresh, lewd, direct, sly, sweet, and honest. Exactly like his writing. He spoke of how Hip Hop had informed his life and work, and how a writer must use experience to shape their art; auto-biography and fiction helix together. His street talk and easy manner reminded me of the slick Mexican kids I grew up with(with due respect for the differences in Latino cultures). No amount of vernacular speech could front on the fact that heâs an insightful and sensitive academic however, which is again mirrored in his writing.<br /><br />âDrownâ depicts poverty and struggle in a matter of fact way. It doesnât inspire pity or admiration, itâs neither saccharine nor acrid; but there are moments of each of these things.To be able to describe finding a pair of jeans with the pockets turned out in a search for whatever by an addict semi-exgirlfriend, and making it sound sort of cute is masterful. The leaky-roofed, chronically hungry, parasite infested childhood sounds almost fun, but yet, you can tell that it isnât. The strangest combination of flavors in the book is the pride/respect/disdain that is held for a father that abandoned his family in the DR for years while farting around up in the US. Because of the archetypical nature of these stories it felt like a wild biography, and I had to remind myself that they were only stories. <br /><br />His writing style, once again very much like his speech, was clear and easy, if a little unconventional, and I had to backpedal here or there to get the gist. Mostly though it flowed so smoothly I was done with it before I realized. A great read.<br /></span>

<div id="review_913802706" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/913802706" />
    <a title="Ana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28781546-ana"><img alt="Ana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450253963p2/28781546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana" href="/user/show/28781546-ana">Ana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28781546-ana?shelf=lgbtqia-character">lgbtqia-character</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer913802706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15921546641541348027">This book is made out of short stories, but they all explore Yunior's experience as a Dominican Republic immigrant, his relationship with his family, the idea of masculinity, race and women. The writing makes this book stand out, Yunior's life being a fairly average one. It just sticks with you and pops in my head unexpectedly. It's not plot heavy, but focuses on the main character and how he interacts with others.<br/><br/>I like how the events are not portrayed in order, because the author uses this to </span>
  <span id="freeText15921546641541348027" style="display:none">This book is made out of short stories, but they all explore Yunior's experience as a Dominican Republic immigrant, his relationship with his family, the idea of masculinity, race and women. The writing makes this book stand out, Yunior's life being a fairly average one. It just sticks with you and pops in my head unexpectedly. It's not plot heavy, but focuses on the main character and how he interacts with others.<br /><br />I like how the events are not portrayed in order, because the author uses this to get us to sympathize with Yunior's pain so that, when we find out that often times he is complicit in the outcome, we still can't hate or dismiss him. I like to compare Ysrael ("he who struggles with God") and Yunior (son struggling with his father's legacy).<br /><br />It's a little silly to rate them individually since they are too connected, but out of force of habit, I did it. <br /><br />"Ysrael" - two brothers are on a mission to unmask Ysrael, who was attacked by a pig as a child; set in the Dominican Republic (3 stars)<br /><br />"Fiesta, 1980" - a son questioning how his father's affair will affect the family reunion; set in the United States (3 stars)<br /><br />"Aurora" - a drug dealer can't forget his drug addicted ex-girlfriend; set in the United States (3 stars)<br /><br />"Aguantando" - scenes depicting family life before the father comes back into their lives; set in the Dominican Republic (4 stars)<br /><br />"Drown" - Yunior's best friend comes back from college and Yunior avoids him; set in the United States (4 stars)<br /><br />"Boyfriend" - Yunior handles the aftermath of his break-up while his neighbors are also breaking up; set in the United States (4 stars)<br /><br />"Edison, New Jersey" - new job and ex-girlfriend story; set in the United States (3 stars)<br /><br />"How to Date a Browngirl, Blackgirl, Whitegirl, or Halfie" - how to bring over girls you won't see again; set in the United States (3 stars)<br /><br />"No Face" - how Ysrael handles the world; set in the Dominican Republic (3 stars)<br /><br />"Negocios" - Yunior's father's imigration story and his messy family life; set in the United States (3 stars)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15921546641541348027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8943897895243373369">Ten short stories about growing up first in the Dominican Republic and then New Jersey. It reminded me a litte of Sherman Alexie's stories, albeit a little less poetic. But still very well done. We discussed "How To Date A Browngirl, Blackgirl, Whitegirl, or Halfie" at particular length in my fiction writing class, so I'll quote one of my favorite bits from that story: <br/>"Clear the government cheese from the refrigerator. If the girl's from the Terrace stack the boxes behind the milk. If she's fro</span>
  <span id="freeText8943897895243373369" style="display:none">Ten short stories about growing up first in the Dominican Republic and then New Jersey. It reminded me a litte of Sherman Alexie's stories, albeit a little less poetic. But still very well done. We discussed "How To Date A Browngirl, Blackgirl, Whitegirl, or Halfie" at particular length in my fiction writing class, so I'll quote one of my favorite bits from that story: <br />"Clear the government cheese from the refrigerator. If the girl's from the Terrace stack the boxes behind the milk. If she's from the Park or Society Hill hide the cheese in the cabinet above the oven, way up where she'll never see. Leave yourself a reminder to get it out before morning or your moms will kick your ass. Take down any embarrassing photos of your family in the campo, especially the one with the half-naked kids dragging a goat on a rope leash. The kids are your cousins and by now they're old enough to understand why you're doing what you're doing." <br /><br />Junot Diaz was one of the guests at my college's writers' festival, and I got to hear him read from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/297673.The_Brief_Wondrous_Life_of_Oscar_Wao" title="The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao by Junot DÃ­az" rel="nofollow noopener">The Brief Wonderous Life of Oscar Wao</a>. The man is <i>hilarious</i> and if you ever get a chance to see him do a reading, for god's sake go. The best part was the question-and-answer session at the end. Someone asked him a really complicated question about his writing process or something, and he spent a minute trying to think of a way to answer that, and then he just laughed and said, "I don't know. Um...fuck, you guys!" It was great. <br /><br />Read for: Intro to Creative Writing</span>

<div id="review_6456856" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6456856" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395296-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206026854p2/395296.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6456856?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/395296-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6456856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17304323986988229337">One of the coolest things that ever happened to me was I got to participate in a creative writing workshop with Junot Diaz. My girlfriend was in the class also, which was the first time we had a class together. We had been living together for a little while, and even though we were very much in love at the time, whe would do certain shit that really got on my nerves, like for example always being late (as in over an hour late!) for everything. So on the first day of class, she came in (predictab</span>
  <span id="freeText17304323986988229337" style="display:none">One of the coolest things that ever happened to me was I got to participate in a creative writing workshop with Junot Diaz. My girlfriend was in the class also, which was the first time we had a class together. We had been living together for a little while, and even though we were very much in love at the time, whe would do certain shit that really got on my nerves, like for example always being late (as in over an hour late!) for everything. So on the first day of class, she came in (predictably) late, and he said something, I forget what, and made her cry. I remember being bummed because I was going to have to deal with the emotional fallout of this after class, but at the same time vindicated, because he finally said what I never had the guys to say.<br /><br />My favorite memories of Junot are of him pleading with students to see a particular point of view, to demonstate it, cries of exultation when they did, his cursing, frustrated stammering, or a telling smile and tilt of the head while he was waiting for someone to finish saying something really stupid. To him, writing was as alive as the most current trend in street art, and that energy was -- out of all his lessons -- the one I took to heart the most.<br /><br />I taught several of the stories from "Drown" in my 10th and 11th grade English classes over at Garfield, and remember them as being some of the more successful experients I undertook while working there. Although they're all connected (and I think "Fiesta, 1980" is the one for which he won the most honors), the story "Ysrael" is my favorite.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17304323986988229337" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_414203989" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414203989" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/414203989?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=u-s-lit">u-s-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=firstreads-wins">firstreads-wins</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer414203989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11012179066043628671">I shelve my fiction alphabetized by authorâs last name, each authorâs works further displayed in chronological publishing order. Presidential biographies start with Washington and travel in order to Obama. Histories stands pretty much as they occurred. Not exactly OCD, but the nuns can certainly be proud of the order they instilled. So I canât explain why, when I open a book of short stories for the first time, I do not read them in order. <br/><br/>I jumped around here, although I did read the final stor</span>
  <span id="freeText11012179066043628671" style="display:none">I shelve my fiction alphabetized by authorâs last name, each authorâs works further displayed in chronological publishing order. Presidential biographies start with Washington and travel in order to Obama. Histories stands pretty much as they occurred. Not exactly OCD, but the nuns can certainly be proud of the order they instilled. So I canât explain why, when I open a book of short stories for the first time, I do not read them in order. <br /><br />I jumped around here, although I did read the final story last. Book closed, I think I would have been better served reading the stories in the order presented. Like <i>Olive Kitteridge</i>, these stories share characters and a family history. They must certainly be semi-autobiographical. They span locations: âNueva Yorkâ and the Dominican. And mostly they are in the voice of âYuniorâ, a young boy with very wide eyes and ears. The language is hip but nevertheless restrained, almost minimalist. The lessons, though, are hardly unique: wounds of childhood, searching for some toehold, loss, fathers and sons. Such a different life told here, and yet I recognized, <i>felt</i>, the shame, the hope, things I can safely predict will eventually wake Diaz in the middle of the night, the wonder of it all.<br /><br />So up you go, between Pete Dexter and Isak Dinesen, right before your later <i>The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao</i>. For no good or special reason. Just because as things unravel, I will know where to find you.<br /><br />Thanks once again to Goodreads Giveaways. Love winning free books.<br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_28159893" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28159893" />
    <a title="Bart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/210421-bart"><img alt="Bart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547689631p2/210421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28159893?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bart" href="/user/show/210421-bart">Bart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who doesn't plan to read Oscar Wao</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28159893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10174527983293991698">If you havenât already read this book, thereâs really no need. Most of its best parts are recycled in <i>Oscar Wao</i>. A man without a face, people shuffling between Santo Domingo and New Jersey, some early experimenting in Junot Diazâs âoriginal voiceâ.<br/><br/>The toughest part of reading Diaz is trying not to put his criticsâ opinions in front of Diazâs words. Trying to separate Diazâs at-times honest efforts from the hysterical effect they have on certain literary types is hard sledding. Itâs not fair to t</span>
  <span id="freeText10174527983293991698" style="display:none">If you havenât already read this book, thereâs really no need. Most of its best parts are recycled in <i>Oscar Wao</i>. A man without a face, people shuffling between Santo Domingo and New Jersey, some early experimenting in Junot Diazâs âoriginal voiceâ.<br /><br />The toughest part of reading Diaz is trying not to put his criticsâ opinions in front of Diazâs words. Trying to separate Diazâs at-times honest efforts from the hysterical effect they have on certain literary types is hard sledding. Itâs not fair to the author to hold othersâ inanities against him â except when it feels likeâs heâs writing for a focus group. Thereâs more of that in <i>Oscar Wao</i> than in <i>Drown</i>, but <i>Oscar Wao</i> casts a cynical eye backwards onto <i>Drown</i>.<br /><br />In this book, Diaz contents himself with being the voice of the Dominican Republic. In <i>Oscar Wao</i>, heâs the voice of Latinos everywhere. That makes this book a somewhat more honest, if less refined, effort.<br /><br />Much of Diazâs success â like most writersâ success â returns, in some part, to good fortune and timing. Diaz wrote <i>Drown</i> at a time when the conversion from literature to cultural commentary was revving up. Who better to marry the two than a talented writer of Dominican roots who told the literary establishment what others were afraid to say? I guess.<br /><br />Since being a Dominican is whatâs most important to Diaz â he does very little with the human condition, otherwise â one should probably rejoice a bit about the circumstances that brought <i>Drown</i> to the public. According to the tired narrative of such things, voices like Diazâs were never given a chance before 1990 or so. If thatâs true then itâs good that <i>Drown</i> was published. Diaz is a talented author who may just surprise us with an enduring work before heâs through.<br /><br />If not, his works will serve as a good counterargument to the next generation of literary types who tell us Latino authors were never given a chance until (insert literary movement here) came along.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_924959185" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/924959185" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6041973-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598551141p2/6041973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/924959185?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/6041973-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6041973-josh?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer924959185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6531047427227132434">An insight into poverty, family matters and ordinary life intertwined amongst several stories. Junot's anecdotes range from the barren streets of Santo Domingo, Dominican Republic in or around the time of Operation Power Pack, the everlasting-but-rewarding fight for the so-called 'American Dream' in Nueva York, Nueva York and a glimpse into the 'la loco' life of teenagers and their vices in Perth Amboy/South Amboy, New Jersey.<br/><br/>The main themes to me are the struggle of a woman forced to ignore her</span>
  <span id="freeText6531047427227132434" style="display:none">An insight into poverty, family matters and ordinary life intertwined amongst several stories. Junot's anecdotes range from the barren streets of Santo Domingo, Dominican Republic in or around the time of Operation Power Pack, the everlasting-but-rewarding fight for the so-called 'American Dream' in Nueva York, Nueva York and a glimpse into the 'la loco' life of teenagers and their vices in Perth Amboy/South Amboy, New Jersey.<br /><br />The main themes to me are the struggle of a woman forced to ignore her husband's infidelities due to a better life for not only her, but her familia; a physically disabled boy who's dreams of being normal doesn't supercede his wish to be a wrestler; the daily lives of Ramon (Yunior) and his brother Rafa in the Capital city of the island -- accepting who they are and living life day by day without worry; a somewhat irresponsible father trying to 'do the right thing', but fails to do so many times until he has no other place to go but in a positive direction.<br /><br />I'm not generally into short stories, but when all seem somewhat random, but really show a complete story in itself, I find an interesting quality about that. This debut from an author, who seemingly came out of nowhere to now, 15+ years later, is appreciated as he should be. This book is rewarding in its purpose, flow and overall language. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6531047427227132434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21808545" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21808545" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1147190-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1234110252p2/1147190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21808545?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/1147190-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21808545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17752769800221224889">WOW! Just freaking wow!!!<br/><br/>I picked this book up because I enjoyed The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao. When I started to read it, I thought that this felt like a handful of failed starts to similar novels. But the further that I read into it, the more I realized what it was that Junot Diaz was doing, painting a complete picture out of multiple fractured pieces. <br/><br/>The writing in this book is remarkably sparse, short with details and full of space where you are asked to interject your own imaginatio</span>
  <span id="freeText17752769800221224889" style="display:none">WOW! Just freaking wow!!!<br /><br />I picked this book up because I enjoyed The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao. When I started to read it, I thought that this felt like a handful of failed starts to similar novels. But the further that I read into it, the more I realized what it was that Junot Diaz was doing, painting a complete picture out of multiple fractured pieces. <br /><br />The writing in this book is remarkably sparse, short with details and full of space where you are asked to interject your own imagination. Diaz fills that space in an odd, non-linear way through the conversation and through the narrative from the characters in completely random fashion. But, the payoff is that as the short stories continue, he has given you enough clues to put everything in perspective.<br /><br />It's then that you realize that the pictures are rarely pretty, often lonely, rich with emptiness and squalor, but not bereft of human spirit clinging to the promise of something...even if all that something is happens to be an empty apartment, a half-filled 40, a broken relationship, or a full syringe.<br /><br />I very much look forward to all his future work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17752769800221224889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100159228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100159228" />
    <a title="Nicholas Armstrong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2593734-nicholas-armstrong"><img alt="Nicholas Armstrong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1284413844p2/2593734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100159228?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Armstrong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Armstrong" href="/user/show/2593734-nicholas-armstrong">Nicholas Armstrong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100159228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8954498481175282550">It's a point of contention with me when authors ignore grammar. That isn't to say I'm against authorial intent -- not using a comma or using one for emphasis -- but when some are just tossed out lackadaisically I have to wonder why. <br/><br/>There are moments in <i>Drown</i> where there is no reason not to use the proper grammar, and being a big fan of the impact grammar can have on a reader (when used correctly) this irks me. Why do (or not do) something if it has no effect? It just seems lazy to me. On the ot</span>
  <span id="freeText8954498481175282550" style="display:none">It's a point of contention with me when authors ignore grammar. That isn't to say I'm against authorial intent -- not using a comma or using one for emphasis -- but when some are just tossed out lackadaisically I have to wonder why. <br /><br />There are moments in <i>Drown</i> where there is no reason not to use the proper grammar, and being a big fan of the impact grammar can have on a reader (when used correctly) this irks me. Why do (or not do) something if it has no effect? It just seems lazy to me. On the other hand, the merging of dialogue into the exposition and description without any quotations does have an interesting affect. I find myself disconnected from the characters and the action; all of the story seemed to just merge into a single melting pot where there was no distinct action or break, just one seamless narrative. This was an interesting choice, but I ultimately prefer the norm. It was too easy for me not to care about anything I was reading when the dialogue was removed and tone was so journalistic to began with.<br /><br />The stories themselves seem to fluctuate wildly in meaning, symbolism, and impact. Some of them are slightly whimsical, some depressing, some highly meaningful, but they are all (it seems) about the same character and his family. I thought it an odd choice that some of the stories would be told in such completely different voices. Yunior seems to waver between tragic hero and heroic villain. His father is much the same; (although ultimately more a villain) a figure of domination and pain upon his family until we are given 'Negocios', which seems to contradict much of what we know of the character. As far as I'd read before the father was an imperceptibly quiet monster with no concern whatsoever for his family or anyone else, and yet he is a young man with hopes and dreams and love. This is all fine, characters just like people can change, but there is no reason given. This character goes from optimistic to abusive in the span of pages and for no concrete reason. I do not think I am naive in thinking there <i>should</i> be a reason for this. I've seen any number of people change and it never seems to be just for the hell of it, so seeing it described in such a way leaves me a little perplexed. Also, I wonder how it is Yunior even knows these stories. His father hardly talked to him, and treated him even less like a son, so I have to wonder how it is Yunior learned any of this from a man who beat his family and cheated openly on their mother. Who would have filled in these details?<br /><br />Perhaps a large majority of my complaints were done purposefully. Perhaps we are not supposed to know what exactly caused the change in Papi or how it is his son came to learn all of this, but dammit, that's stupid. I <i>want</i> to know that. Assuming this was actually Diaz, he knows, so why not tell us? Without the details these stories fluctuate in quality and cohesion and I can't think it would have harmed a thing to have let us in on the how and why.<br /><br />It's an interesting style, but not, ultimately, one I am a fan of. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8954498481175282550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_821413836" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/821413836" />
    <a title="Faisal Pakkali" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27614962-faisal-pakkali"><img alt="Faisal Pakkali" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389556363p2/27614962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/821413836?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faisal Pakkali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faisal Pakkali" href="/user/show/27614962-faisal-pakkali">Faisal Pakkali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer821413836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14416999486921597559">After reading the first short story in Junot Diazâs collection âDrownâ, I went on to the internet to browse for reviews. To be frank, I didnât understand any of them. The reviews talk about issues of identity in the context of the immigrant experience and all that jazz. However the story still stuck with me despite my lack of knowledge of the Dominican Republic and the urban communities of New Jersey.<br/><br/>The story is basically about two brothers, Rafa and Yunior who become fascinated by a boy named </span>
  <span id="freeText14416999486921597559" style="display:none">After reading the first short story in Junot Diazâs collection âDrownâ, I went on to the internet to browse for reviews. To be frank, I didnât understand any of them. The reviews talk about issues of identity in the context of the immigrant experience and all that jazz. However the story still stuck with me despite my lack of knowledge of the Dominican Republic and the urban communities of New Jersey.<br /><br />The story is basically about two brothers, Rafa and Yunior who become fascinated by a boy named Ysrael. The boy had been attacked by a pig when he was a child, his face brutally mangled. He is notorious for his grotesque appearance throughout the area and hides his face with a mask. Children often attack and insult him, both frightened and scornful of him. Rafa and Yunior go on a trip to meet Ysrael and possibly see his face. A violent episode ensues.<br /><br />The most immediate reason for my attraction towards this story is the prose. Rich and poetic yet spare and straightforward, the Spanish words that lace the prose, only adds to it an exotic quality. The second reason would be that I, as a reader, share the main characterâs fascination with poor Ysraelâs involuntary promise of the grotesque. However the story is not simply a catchy tale told in beautiful prose. It seems rich with meaning under the surface of itâs simple narrative.<br /><br />The story is divided into six small parts and details the lives of the boys in the countryside (campo) and also introduces and elaborates on the character of Ysrael. The crux of the story is the two boysâ quest to find out what is under his mask.<br /><br />Funnily enough, the only truly sympathetic character is Ysrael. Rafa is shown to be somewhat violent and unscrupulous, while Yunior is cowardly. When they meet him, Ysrael is shown to be an outcast not only because of his face and because of his huge size, but even in his dressing. He is dressed completely in âNorth-Americanâ clothes that his father sends him regularly. Rafa has only one set of these that he wears every time he goes to meet girls.<br /><br />The most important issue here is of course the mask. Itâs purpose is to hide something terrible, but in itself becomes a symbol, a herald of this terrible thing. So the mask is a failure. It betrays Ysrael by attracting as much attention as his face would. It teases the onlookers, tantalizing them into wondering what is under the mask. It may even make him seem more monstrous than he really is. The moment when his face is described in the story and given fixed definition is actually quite anticlimactic to the reader, even though it admittedly, scares Yunior.<br /><br />I found myself wondering this while reading the story: Would the world end if Ysrael walked out with his bare face for everyone to see?<br /><br />Maybe I am being naive. Maybe the world would end for Ysrael. But the mask that he wears seems more trouble than itâs worth. And maybe thatâs what the story could be about. The consequences of Ysrael hiding who he really is versus the shame and ostracism associated with his identity.<br /><br />And there is more to it actually. At the closing of the story Rafa and Yunior have a conversation about Ysraelâs face. Yunior holds out hope that things will get better for Ysrael and that medical help would be able to do something for him. Yunior shows more sympathy towards Ysrael than Rafa. Perhaps the timid Yunior felt a kinship towards the outcast Ysrael. And perhaps he, like Ysrael, hopes or a better future. However Rafa issues a warning to him. There is no point in running away from who you really are.<br /><br />*<br /><br />Ysrael will be Ok, I said.<br /><br />Donât bet on it.<br /><br />Theyâre going to fix him.<br /><br />A muscle fluttered between his jaw bone and his ear. Yunior, he said tiredly. They arenât going to do shit to him.<br /><br />How do you know?<br /><br />I know, he said.<br /><br />*</span>

<div id="review_300261792" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/300261792" />
    <a title="Jinny Chung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2277187-jinny-chung"><img alt="Jinny Chung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295599397p2/2277187.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300261792?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jinny Chung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jinny Chung" href="/user/show/2277187-jinny-chung">Jinny Chung</a>
      </span>

        rated it

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2277187-jinny-chung?shelf=love-love-love">love-love-love</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer300261792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6476032003417318947">I feel a closeness to Junot DÃ­az that I don't with most authors. There's something so familiar and inviting about his prose; when I read it, I'm transported there. "I can totally hear him saying that!" The people in his novels are So Real to me, and when he talks coming-of-age, all his characters are versions of the people from my childhood.<br/><br/>Junot, please write more. I require more than just this and <i>The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao</i>. How it is you make us laugh and cry as cathartically as you</span>
  <span id="freeText6476032003417318947" style="display:none">I feel a closeness to Junot DÃ­az that I don't with most authors. There's something so familiar and inviting about his prose; when I read it, I'm transported there. "I can totally hear him saying that!" The people in his novels are So Real to me, and when he talks coming-of-age, all his characters are versions of the people from my childhood.<br /><br />Junot, please write more. I require more than just this and <i>The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao</i>. How it is you make us laugh and cry as cathartically as you do, I'll never know. How, motherf*cker, how?!<br /><br />"Everyone had a different opinion on the damage. TÃ­o said it wasn't bad but the father was very sensitive about anything taunting his oldest son, which explained the mask. TÃ­a said that if we were to look on his face we would be sad for the rest of our lives. That's why the poor boy's mother spends her day in church. I had never been sad more than a few hours and the thought of that sensation lasting a lifetime scared the hell out of me."<br /><br />.<br /><br />"Mami must have caught me studying her because she stopped what she was doing and gave me a smile, maybe her first one of the night. Suddenly I wanted to go over and hug her, for no other reason than I loved her, but there were about eleven fat jiggling bodies between us. So I sat down on the tiled floor and waited."<br /><br />.<br /><br />"We could never get Mami to do anything after work, even cook dinner, if she didn't first sit awhile in her rocking chair. She didn't want to hear nothing about our problems, the scratches we'd put into our knees, who said what. She'd sit on the back patio with her eyes closed and let the bugs bite mountains onto her arms and legs. Sometimes I climbed the guanÃ¡bana tree and when she'd open her eyes and catch me smiling down on her, she'd close them again and I would drop twigs onto her until she laughed."<br /><br />.<br /><br />"Homegirl was too beautiful, too high-class for a couple of knuckleheads for us. Never saw her in a t-shirt or without jewelry. And her boyfriend, olvÃ­date. That nigger could have been a model; hell, they both could have been models, which was what they probably were, considering that I never heard word one pass between them about a job or a fucking boss. People like these were untouchables to me, raised on some other planet and then transplanted into my general vicinity to remind me how bad I was living."</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37256808-tim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1264006306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4399834852157837503">Here is another literary star that has gotten a lot of attention in recent years. Diaz is a Dominican-American, raised in Washington Heights, NYC. This story cycle centers on one Dominican family that is split between Upper Manhattan and Santo Domingo. Each story shows a different facet of the life of this family, with the focus primarily on the young man known as Junior (or âYuniorâ). This is really good, enjoyable writing, even if some of it does seem to have the stamp of an MFA writing studen</span>
  <span id="freeText4399834852157837503" style="display:none">Here is another literary star that has gotten a lot of attention in recent years. Diaz is a Dominican-American, raised in Washington Heights, NYC. This story cycle centers on one Dominican family that is split between Upper Manhattan and Santo Domingo. Each story shows a different facet of the life of this family, with the focus primarily on the young man known as Junior (or âYuniorâ). This is really good, enjoyable writing, even if some of it does seem to have the stamp of an MFA writing studentâs work. He also uses the no quotation marks style that Irvine Welsh and others have used. Each story is rich in detail, but clean and sharp, and makes its points and moves on, without overstatement. The whole thing has a real feeling to it--not just because of the gritty homeboy/homegirl environment of many of the pieces but because there is believable emotion interwoven through each piece. <br /><br />A couple of the stories feature a teenager called âNo Face,â a disfigured kid who had his face bitten by a pig when he was an infant and no wears a cloth face mask. Of course he is treated mercilessly by the other kids. âAuroraâ is a nice one, about the love between a young drug dealer and his messed up on and off girlfriend. âAguantandoâ is about a young boy waiting to hear from his father, who left the family in DR and went to New York. âDrownâ takes a look at homo/bi issues in the hood, and centers on a masculine young man who is avoiding seeing a former best buddy, apparently because of the couple of sexual experiences they had together. âEdison, New Jerseyâ is the surprisingly entertaining tale of a young guy who delivers pool tables for a living. âHow to Date a Browngirl, Blackgirlâ¦â is an amusing look at a young guyâs seduction techniques and experiences. âNegociosâ is about the struggle of a newly arrived immigrant in the USA. This is good stuff all the way through.<br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4399834852157837503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Deb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5276322-deb"><img alt="Deb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526245087p2/5276322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1281853062?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deb" href="/user/show/5276322-deb">Deb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1281853062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4854309799740712559"><i>
  <b>Kinda funny. Very stereotypical</b>
</i><br/><br/>This was a short story. I ran across a podcast of the author reading it as a part of a radio series broadcast back in 1995 by <i>The Newyorker and decided to give it a listen.</i> <br/><br/>This is a guide on how to date girls from the perspective of a teenage Dominican boy living in New Jersey. So on that note, it's not really a guide but a point of view. It's actually more like what this kid, who thinks he is some suave expert would tell his boys about dating high school girl</span>
  <span id="freeText4854309799740712559" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Kinda funny. Very stereotypical</b>
</i><br /><br />This was a short story. I ran across a podcast of the author reading it as a part of a radio series broadcast back in 1995 by <i>The Newyorker and decided to give it a listen.</i> <br /><br />This is a guide on how to date girls from the perspective of a teenage Dominican boy living in New Jersey. So on that note, it's not really a guide but a point of view. It's actually more like what this kid, who thinks he is some suave expert would tell his boys about dating high school girls. I think he considers himself some up and coming Casanova and this is his advice. The diversity of the neighborhood calls for him to describe three different scenarios for three different types of girls, Black girls, white girls and what he calls "halfies", mixed race girls. It seems he considers himself an "equal opportunity" young player. This short story is filled with a young mans limited, stereotypical, amateur predictions of how each racial delegate of the female sex will respond to his carefully thought out applications of immature charm. <br /><br />It's funny, because as an adult I want to say, "Oh little boy please." But thinking backward I know this is how some teenage boys thought. I only hope that most of them outgrew all of this as they matured. I don't necessarily find the cultural portrayals correct, however I'm not from the authors neighborhood so what do I know. <br /><br />An interesting little peek into a mind in a point of time. I recommend it. Three stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4854309799740712559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2963863705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2963863705" />
    <a title="Ethan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79606570-ethan"><img alt="Ethan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604543009p2/79606570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2963863705?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ethan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ethan" href="/user/show/79606570-ethan">Ethan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2963863705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3071000273397195261"><i>Drown</i> is a series of short stories that are semi-autobiographical and that essentially deal with life in the Dominican Republic and with immigrating from there to America to achieve the "American Dream". After reading it, I can't help the feeling that the author somewhat hates his country of birth.<br/><br/>I say that because almost every character is portrayed as an absolutely despicable person. The stories are filled with scuzzy drug dealers, siblings who beat up deformed children, deadbeat fathers, sha</span>
  <span id="freeText3071000273397195261" style="display:none"><i>Drown</i> is a series of short stories that are semi-autobiographical and that essentially deal with life in the Dominican Republic and with immigrating from there to America to achieve the "American Dream". After reading it, I can't help the feeling that the author somewhat hates his country of birth.<br /><br />I say that because almost every character is portrayed as an absolutely despicable person. The stories are filled with scuzzy drug dealers, siblings who beat up deformed children, deadbeat fathers, shady businessmen, con artists, etc. On top of all that, every character seems to be OK with being such, offering the reader little hope the stories will turn in a positive direction (they almost never do).<br /><br />This book is well-written, and the author is clearly pouring his heart out. I understand what he is trying to achieve, and on some level I think he succeeds, but it's all just so terribly, terribly depressing and completely unenjoyable as a reading experience.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3071000273397195261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cUAfLpQXfQqbYodtE1KUJKZgyEHKXrlwooPaOXo0MVF4vJ5Kb8PjWB3aCcIkLI4LBZBd7DDI+5uFoaNwyTTw8g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('72R3a/RC1Z6rpVJxIkZ/+NGKyp6MErUPCAgc9O9PqY/mmPYPD5ZLzC0d3N4VOGXXcnpfM3aE9+QvKmW9XE9oLA==')})
  }

A skillful blend of fact and fiction, In the Time of the Butterflies is inspired by the true story of the three Mirabal sisters who, in 1960, were murdered for their part in an underground plot to overthrow the government. Alvarez breathes life into these historical figures--known as &quot;las mariposas,&quot; or &quot;the butterflies,&quot; in the underground--as she imagines their teenage years, their gradual involvement with the revolution, and their terror as their dissentience is uncovered.  Alvarez\'s controlled writing perfectly captures the mounting tension as &quot;the butterflies&quot; near their horrific end. The novel begins with the recollections of Dede, the fourth and surviving sister, who fears abandoning her routines and her husband to join the movement. Alvarez also offers the perspectives of the other sisters: brave and outspoken Minerva, the family\'s political ringleader; pious Patria, who forsakes her faith to join her sisters after witnessing the atrocities of the tyranny; and the baby sister, sensitive Maria Teresa, who, in a series of diaries, chronicles her allegiance to Minerva and the physical and spiritual anguish of prison life.   <span id="freeText6639521023243013451" style="display:none">Junot DÃ­az was born in the Dominican Republic and raised in New Jersey. He is the author of the critically acclaimed Drown; The Brief Wondrous Life of Oscar Wao, which won the 2008 Pulitzer Prize and the National Book Critics Circle Award; and This Is How You Lose Her, a New York Times bestseller and National Book Award finalist. He is the recipient of a MacArthur âGeniusâ Fellowship, PEN/Malamud Award, Dayton Literary Peace Prize, Guggenheim Fellowship, and PEN/O. Henry Award. A graduate of Rutgers College, DÃ­az is currently the fiction editor at Boston Review and the Rudge and Nancy Allen Professor of Writing at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.<br /></span>
